(Adds detail, background)
BRUSSELS (Dow Jones)--The  European Commission will Wednesday fine the world's biggest computer chip maker,  Intel Corp. (INTC), for breaking European antitrust rules, said people familiar with the matter Friday.

TD
The commission will also tell  Intel to make changes to the way it provides rebates to computer manufacturers, the people said.
The commission officials have spent most of Friday discussing the  Intel fine with national
competition authorities, which according to E.U. procedure also have a say on the matter, the people
familiar with the case said.

They added that once all the stakeholders have had their say on the  fine, the final document will be presented to the college of commissioners Wednesday, which is then likely to adopt it.

An Intel spokesman declined to comment, only saying that at this point a pending decision still represents just rumors and speculation. A commission spokesman also declined to comment.

Intel's trouble with the commission dates from 2000, when rival chip maker Advanced Micro Devices
Inc. (AMD), of Sunnyvale, Calif. filed a complaint saying  Intel was blocking its access to the market.

In 2007, the E.U. sent preliminary charges to  Intel, alleging that  Intel kept AMD out of the microprocessor market by paying computer manufacturers rebates to keep their usage of AMD chips down and by selling chips below cost to thwart AMD from swooping on key accounts.

Last year, the E.U. added more charges, alleging that  Intel paid a major retailer to keep computers that use AMD chips off its shelves.

The commission's Wednesday decision is said to be extremely complex and lengthy, in order to safeguard the  antitrust enforcer against any possible legal challenges from  Intel, which is likely facing one of the highest  fines in Europe's  antitrust history, Brussels-based lawyers say.

The E.U.'s  competition commissioner, Neelie Kroes, can under the commission's rules  fine Intel up to 10% of its annual revenue, which in 2008 was almost $38 billion.

Last year, the commission issued a record $1.16-billion  fine against Microsoft Corp. for non-compliance in the E.U.'s long-running  antitrust fight with the software giant.
-By Peppi Kiviniemi, Dow Jones Newswires; +32 (0)2 741 1483; peppi.kiviniemi@dowjones.com
(Charles Forelle contributed to this article) [ 08-05-09 1533GMT ]

HD           Chipmaker  Intel faces fine of up to &euro;1bn if found guilty  of trying  to alienate competition
WC          407 words
PD           9 May 2009
SN           The Irish Examiner
SC           IRISEX
LA            English

LP
THE country’s largest microchip maker,  Intel is facing massive fines of up to &euro;1 billion for allegedly trying to put its competitors out of business.

Intel Ireland employs over 5,500 people directly and indirectly at Leixlip, which is its largest manufacturing plant outside the US, and has over 80% of the world market for CPU computer chips.

TD
The European Commission has said that  Intel was trying to unfairly kill off  competition from AMD (Advanced Micro Devices) by offering rebates in exchange for using  Intel-only products, by selling at below-cost to strategic customers such as governments when bidding against AMD, and making payments to delay or cancel products using AMD chips.

About four years ago  competition officials from the  European Commission raided Intel offices in a number of European locations, though not in Ireland, and have been building a case against the US company.

Now competition commissioner Neelie Kroes is expected to bring her proposals to the weekly meeting of commissioners on Wednesday.

Charlie McCreevy, commissioner for the internal market, is expected to argue against massive  fines, pointing out that  Intel is a good employer in Ireland.

However, the German Commissioner Gunter Verhoygen, who is in charge of enterprise and industry, is expected to speak against  Intel as their main rival, AMD, is German based.
Ms Kroes’ cabinet, headed up by Irish lawyer Anthony Whelan, is likely to decide the size of the  fine
on Wednesday evening.

The commission said Intel’s abusive behaviour began in 2003 and continued for about three years. During that time AMD took market share from  Intel when their new generation of microprocessors outperformed  Intel’s chips.

Intel has said that its behaviour has been lawful, good for  competition and has benefited customers.

Microsoft was  fined around $500 million four years ago for unfair practices but the rules on  fines have changed since and the size can now be calculated on the size of the market affected, the duration of the offence and how severe its actions were on the  competition.
In this instance it looks likely that the commission will go for a significant sum like &euro;1bn. A look at
Intel’s balance sheet shows that it made a net profit of $647m for the first quarter this year and has
$14bn in cash.

* EU to say  Intel paid PC firms to delay rival products-sources * To say  Intel gave retailers inducement to exclude AMD-sources * EU sees acts as "naked restrictions" on  competition-sources

TD
By David Lawsky

AMSTERDAM, May 10 (Reuters) - EU  antitrust regulators are expected to say this week that  Intel Corp illegally paid computer makers to postpone or cancel the launch of products containing chips made by its main rival, sources familiar with the case said on Sunday.

The European Commission is set to decide on Wednesday to  fine the world's largest chipmaker and order changes to its business practices for what the EU executive sees as "naked restrictions" to competition, the sources said.

There was no indication of how big a  fine might be levied. The largest  fine levied by the EC for an abuse of market dominance was the 497 million euros ($655 million) demanded from Microsoft on March 24, 2004.

The sources said the Brussels-based Commission is expected to rule that  Intel committed two violations in which the firm abused its dominance of the market for central processing units, the chips at the heart of the world's 1 billion personal computers.

The EU executive will say  Intel gave rebates to computer makers to restrict or eliminate the use of chips made by its rival, Advanced Micro Devices, and provided other inducements to retailers to sell only machines with  Intel CPUs.

In its ruling the Commission will order  Intel to end by a specific date those rebates which it deems to be illegal, the sources said.

In its second finding, the Commission will say  Intel paid PC makers to delay or scrap the launch of products containing AMD chips. The Commission will characterise the payments as "naked restrictions" to  competition, the sources said.

The Commission will state that the violations occurred during a period stretching back eight years, they said.

In committing the first violation,  Intel set percentages of its own chips that it wanted PC makers to use, the sources said.
For example, NEC Corp was told that 20 percent of its desktop and notebook machines could have
AMD chips, the sources said.

All Lenovo notebooks had to use  Intel chips, as did relevant Dell products. The figure was 95 percent for Hewlett-Packard's business desktops, they said.

European Commission spokesman Jonathan Todd said the commission had no comment.  Intel
spokesman Chuck Mulloy, speaking by telephone from California also declined to comment.
Page 41 of 226 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
The EU executive charged  Intel in 2007 over illegal rebates to computer makers. In July last year, it charged the firm with paying retailers not to sell PCs using AMD chips.

Intel has said repeatedly it has done nothing wrong. (Editing by Dale Hudson and Greg Mahlich) ($1=.7592 euros)

HD     EU antitrust ruling  against Intel due on Wednesday
BY      jeevan@cpidubai.com (Staff)
WC     509 words
PD      10 May 2009
SN      Network World Middle East
SC      NETWO
LA      English
CY      © 2009 IDG Middle East. All Rights Reserved. Provided by Syndigate.info, an Albawaba.com company

LP
The European Commission is expected to make one of the most significant antitrust decisions in its history on Wednesday when it punishes computer chip-maker  Intel for stifling competition from smaller rivals.

The official line is that the case is still ongoing, but one person close to the  competition department said on condition of anonymity on Friday that the 27 commissioners will conclude the case, which has been under investigation since 2000, at their next weekly meeting on Wednesday.

TD
Intel is the latest giant from the IT industry to be slapped down by Europe's top  competition regulator. Like Microsoft five years ago and IBM in the 1980s,  Intel claims it is simply doing what any company would, only better.

While IBM settled with the regulator, agreeing to change the way it competed in the market for mainframe computers, Microsoft and  Intel have stuck to their guns. The two have applied similar strategies to dealing with the pesky authorities based in Brussels.

Consequently Microsoft was  fined $663.4 million for abusing its dominant position in the software market, plus an additional  fine for failing to respect the  antitrust ruling.

Intel faces a fine of a similar order of magnitude for illegally handing out rebates to computer manufacturers in return for them buying the bulk of their x86 computer processing chips (CPUs) from Intel, the Commission is expected to say next week.

The company also stands accused of paying computer makers for scrapping or delaying the launch of machines fitted with chips made by its nearest rival AMD, and of selling its chips for server computers at below cost to large customers such as governments and universities.

Last year the Commission added fresh charges, accusing the chip giant of paying generous rebates to Media Markt, Europe's biggest chain of IT stores, in return for it de-listing all computers running AMD chips.

Intel dominates the personal-computer chip market by a mile. At the end of last year its market share stood at 81.9%, while AMD held 17.7%, according to IDC.

Technically, the Commission can fine a company up to 10% of its global sales the year before a monopoly abuse ruling. Last year Intel generated revenues of $37.6 billion, which means the ceiling for a fine would be $3.76 billion. But the regulator has never applied a maximum  fine to date.

The company has not been informed that a ruling is due next week. "We haven't heard anything officially, just rumors mostly coming from journalists," said  Intel spokesman Robert Manetta.

And he repeated the company's standard line that its conduct is lawful, pro-competitive and beneficial to consumers.

Europe isn't the only region where Intel has run into trouble with antitrust authorities. In 2005 the company settled with Japan's competition office. Last year it was fined nearly $20 million in South Korea. Meanwhile, the company is under investigation in the U.S. by the Federal Trade Commission.
CO     intl : Intel Corporation

HD        Intel braced for competition fine
BY        By Nikki Tait in Brussels and Chris Nuttall in San Francisco
WC      755 words
PD       10 May 2009
SN       Financial Times (FT.Com)
SC       FTCOM
LA        English
CY       (c) 2009 The Financial Times Limited. All rights reserved

LP
Intel, the world's biggest chipmaker, is bracing itself this week for one of the heaviest penalties ever levied in Europe for anti-competitive behaviour.

The European Union is expected to decide to take action against  Intel at a weekly meeting of commissioners on Wednesday - the climax of a decade-long investigation into the company's marketing practices.

TD
Some lawyers suggest that the record penalties heaped on Microsoft, which combined have topped the
€1bn mark during the course of its long battle with Brussels, could be approached or even exceeded,
although others are more cautious.

While the size of the  fine may dominate the headlines, many say the  European Commission's ability to order corrective measures could be more damaging to  Intel in the long run, forcing it to change the way
it does business.

Intel has consistently denied behaving illegally. "Our business practices are lawful, pro-competitive and good for consumers," it maintains.

In choosing how to enforce a "cease and desist" order, the EU regulator will have to confront the complex web of incentives and rebates offered by  Intel to customers that have helped it dominate the market for PC microprocessors.

The Intel investigation dates back to 2000, when its closest rival Advanced Micro Devices filed a complaint that it was being driven out of the market by  Intel's practices . But it only gathered steam some four years later, when officials requested fresh information from the chipmaker and other market players.
A year after that, commission officials carried out a series of raids to get more information, and, in July
2007, the chipmaker was formally charged with abusing its dominant market position and offering illegal
rebates to computer manufacturers, which allegedly shut AMD out of the market.

The complaint was then bolstered last year when, after more raids, the commission also accused the company of using rebates to persuade a leading European retailer to sell only  Intel-based personal computers.
In theory, if the commission's final decision does go against the company, the  fine could be as much as
10 per cent of  Intel's annual worldwide revenues.
These totalled $37.6bn in 2008, meaning the maximum level could approach $4bn.

But that 10 per cent level is rarely reached, with commission guidelines now trying to tie  fines much more closely to the scale of sales affected by the anti-competitive conduct and the length of time the offences persisted.

However, the betting is still on a heavy penalty, given the charges cover several years' behaviour in a very large market and competition officials' insistence that  fines need to both "deter and punish".


Page 45 of 226 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Intel, which has maintained a market share of around 80 per cent in sales of computer microprocessors worldwide, would have no problems finding the money - it had $10.3bn on hand in cash and securities at the end of the first quarter.

What will concern it more is any change to long-established business practices forced on it by a "cease and desist" order. Just how that might be structured by the commission is the subject of much debate in Brussels' legal circles - with views varying on how much detail theregulator will seek to lay out, and whether it might deploy a "monitoring trustee" to oversee compliance. This structure was used, but not to huge applause, in the Microsoft case.

Intel, for its part, refuses to discuss whether it would try to appeal an adverse decision. To date, its response to other regulators' action has varied: Japan's Fair Trade Commission ruled in 2005 that it had abused its monopoly power and the company accepted a "cease and desist" order but did not accept guilt or pay a fine.

Then in June last year, South Korea's Fair Trade Commission  fined Intel Won26bn for abusing its dominant market position in that country and offering discounts to two Korean PC makers in an attempt to drive AMD out of the market. This decision is under appeal.

But its response to any adverse finding could be heavily shaped by developments back in its home country. Last year, the Federal Trade Commission launched a formal  antitrust investigation, following a similar move by the New York attorney-general. And the company also faces a civil damages case brought by AMD in the Delaware courts, which is due to go to trial next year - not to mention a number of class-action lawsuits.

HD     Tech firms could see fallout  from antitrust shift
BY     By JORDAN ROBERTSON
CR     AP Technology Writer
WC    890 words
PD     12 May 2009
ET      12:24 AM
SN     Associated Press Newswires
SC     APRS
LA      English
CY     (c) 2009. The Associated Press. All Rights Reserved.

LP
SAN FRANCISCO (AP) - If the Obama administration is serious about more aggressively responding to antitrust complaints, some of technology's biggest companies could have to rethink their business strategies or expansion plans.

The administration said Monday it has abandoned Bush-era policies that it criticized as too friendly to companies that dominate their markets. The Justice Department didn't call out any companies by name, and its shift in approach will affect all industries. But it raises the stakes for tech heavyweights whose practices have been questioned elsewhere more than in the United States.

TD
For instance,  Intel Corp. could face a steep  fine in Europe this week over its behavior in the microprocessor industry. An IBM Corp. competitor is accusing the company in Europe of manipulating the market for mainframe computers. Microsoft Corp. -- a marquee  antitrust defendant during the Clinton administration -- has been battling other charges in Europe in this decade as well.

Tougher antitrust enforcement could also focus on Google Inc., whose leading market shares in online search and advertising markets were already drawing scrutiny in the waning days of the Bush administration.

It remains to be seen whether the Justice Department's different approach will lead to more court cases or more challenges to proposed business deals. But  antitrust lawyers say a more aggressive antitrust philosophy likely would make the Obama administration resemble the Clinton years far more than the
Bush years.

"This is back to the future," said Stephen Bomse, partner in the  antitrust group of the Orrick, Herrington & Sutcliffe law firm.

Under Clinton, the Justice Department brought 12 anti-monopoly cases that didn't involve mergers or acquisitions. That included the long-running case against Microsoft in which a judge at one point ordered Microsoft split into two. (The case eventually was settled with the Bush administration, though it lives on: The Justice Department recently extended its oversight of Microsoft's compliance with the settlement for another 18 months, into 2011).

Under Bush, the department brought three anti-monopoly cases, but none outside of mergers and acquisitions. The targets in those cases were Chicago-based industrial component supplier Amsted Industries Inc., newspaper publishers Daily Gazette Co. and MediaNews Group Inc., and chip maker Microsemi Corp.

Robert Pitofsky, chairman of the Federal Trade Commission from 1995 until 2001 and now an attorney with the Arnold & Porter law firm, said he doesn't think the U.S. will entirely match Europe's zeal for antitrust cases.

One reason is that the U.S. is still more likely to have a free-market bent than Europe, he said. And it can be tricky to regulate technology from an  antitrust perspective. Technological innovations can create

Page 47 of 226 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
monopolists very quickly by cornering or creating specific parts of a market. If regulators are too aggressive, they run the risk of stifling innovation.

"The vast majority of activity in Silicon Valley is innovation, trying to make a better product -- that was not viewed as anticompetitive under Bush or Clinton," said Jonathan Jacobson, a partner in the  antitrust practice at the Wilson Sonsini Goodrich & Rosati law firm. "Technological innovation is supposed to keep you out of trouble."

But the standard that tech companies are judged by -- whether they are engaging in "exclusionary" or "predatory" conduct against competitors -- is the same as everyone else. Viewed through that lens, tech companies can be at a disadvantage because they grow so fast and can get on regulators' radars much more quickly.

Already fears of a tougher  antitrust regime under Obama might have played out in Silicon Valley. When IBM was discussing a proposed $7 billion takeover of rival Sun Microsystems Inc. this spring, Sun sought detailed assurances that IBM would see the deal through an  antitrust review. When the talks collapsed, Sun ended up agreeing to be acquired by Oracle Corp., a company with less overlap with Sun, meaning the deal is perceived as less likely to generate  antitrust questions.

Intel's antitrust challenges arose from complaints by smaller rival Advanced Micro Devices Inc. According to AMD,  Intel boxed AMD out of certain accounts by paying computer makers and retailers to avoid AMD's products. AMD claims that  Intel would drop this hammer when AMD's market share would get too big.

Intel, which owns about 80 percent of the worldwide PC microprocessor market, argues that the rebates it offered were legal, and are a common way to reward buying in very high volumes.

European regulators don't buy  Intel's argument. They have charged  Intel with engaging in a coordinated strategy to limit AMD's reach, including by selling server chips below the cost of making them. The European Commission is expected to formally  fine Intel this week. The  fines can be up to 10 percent of a company's annual revenue, and in 2008,  Intel had $37.6 billion in worldwide sales.

The U.S. initially stayed on the sidelines while  Intel and AMD began duking it out overseas in 2003. Last year U.S. regulators stepped up their inquiries, with the Federal Trade Commission opening a formal probe into Intel's sales tactics. The FTC's two-year investigation had been considered "informal" until that point. So far, the U.S. Justice Department has not taken action.

EU antitrust regulators are expected to find Intel guilty of anti-competitive practices by offering discounts to computer manufacturers for using Intel chips as well as encouraging them not to use hardware from rival AMD.

The European Commission has been investigating the chip giant since the year 2000 for illegally paying off manufacturers to postpone and, in some cases, even cancel the launch of products made with its rival’s chips.

TD
Now sources familiar with the case told Reuters

that regulators are expected to announce on May 13th that  Intel Corp has been found guilty and will be slapped with a fine for violating  antitrust policies.

As an example, sources said that NEC Corp was told only 20% of its desktop and notebook products could have AMD chips. Additionally, all Lenovo notebooks and about 95% of all Hewlett-Packard's business had to use only chips from  Intel.

The highest  fine ever levied by the EU for such behaviour was the 2004 ruling against Microsoft, where the tech giant was  fined $655 million

HD     Brussels to find Intel anti-competitive
WC     414 words
PD      11 May 2009
SN      Business and Finance Daily News Service
SC      BNFD
LA      English
CY      Copyright (c) 2009, Media World Ltd

LP
EU antitrust regulators are expected to say this week that  Intel illegally paid computer makers to postpone or cancel the launch of products containing chips made by its main rival, sources familiar with the case have said.

The European Commission is set to decide on Wednesday to  fine the world's largest chipmaker and order changes to its business practices for what the EU executive sees as "naked restrictions" to competition, the sources said.

TD
There was no indication of how big a  fine might be levied. The largest  fine levied by the EC for an  abuse
of market dominance was the 497 million euro demanded from Microsoft on March 24, 2004.

The sources said the Brussels-based Commission is expected to rule that  Intel committed two violations in which the firm abused its dominance of the market for central processing units, the chips at the heart of the world's 1 billion personal computers. The EU executive will say  Intel gave rebates to computer
makers to restrict or eliminate the use of chips made by its rival, Advanced Micro Devices, and provided other inducements to retailers to sell only machines with  Intel CPUs. In its ruling the Commission will order Intel to end by a specific date those rebates which it deems to be illegal, the sources said. In its second finding, the Commission will say  Intel paid PC makers to delay or scrap the launch of products containing AMD chips. The Commission will characterise the payments as "naked restrictions" to competition, the sources said. The Commission will state that the violations occurred during a period stretching back eight years, they said. In committing the first violation,  Intel set percentages of its own
chips that it wanted PC makers to use, the sources said. For example, NEC Corp was told that 20 percent of its desktop and notebook machines could have AMD chips, the sources said. All Lenovo notebooks
had to use Intel chips, as did relevant Dell products. The figure was 95 percent for Hewlett-Packard's business desktops, they said.  European Commission spokesman Jonathan Todd said the commission had no comment.  Intel spokesman Chuck Mulloy, speaking by telephone from California also declined to comment. The EU executive charged  Intel in 2007 over illegal rebates to computer makers. In July last year, it charged the firm with paying retailers not to sell PCs using AMD chips.  Intel has said repeatedly it has done nothing wrong. (c) Reuters

TOP STORIES

RAFT OF FINANCIAL FIRMS UNVEIL STOCK SALES

A handful of financial companies announce plans to collectively sell more than $7 billion in stock as they look to raise cash during a period of investor interest, with some companies using the proceeds to pay back funds from the TARP.

TD



US STOCK FUTURES DROP AFTER STRONG WEEK

U.S. stock futures are lower as traders take profits following another strong week for stocks in which the S&P 500 rose another 6%, boosted by hopes that the economy will climb out of recession and that banks will largely survive the financial crisis.



US PLAYED ROUGH WITH CHRYSLER'S CREDITORS

President Barack Obama's auto task force heard a blunt message early this spring from J.P. Morgan Chase & Co., the largest lender to Chrysler. In any deal to remake the troubled auto maker, Chrysler would have to repay its lenders all $6.9 billion it owed.



HEDGE FUNDS RAISE VOICES, AND RETURNS

Hedge-fund managers are showing rare public outrage against the Obama administration, saying that it has wrongly rebuked investors necessary to salving the financial crisis.



DISH NETWORK PROFIT RISES 21%; ECHOSTAR SWINGS TO LOSS

Dish Network says its 1Q earnings rose to $312.7 million, or 70c a share, despite another drop in subscribers, while former unit EchoStar swung to a loss of $645,000, or a penny a share, on falling revenue.



ACKMAN MAKES CASE TO CHANGE TARGET BOARD



Page 51 of 226 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Hedge-fund mogul calls a meeting in Manhattan today to introduce his slate of five dissident directors, including himself, that he's asking shareholders to elect May 28.



GM HIRES SEARCH FIRM FOR NEW BOARD

General Motors has hired an executive-search firm to help find replacements for at least half of its 12 directors, reflecting the Obama administration's increasing influence over the auto maker.



BOFA SEEKS BUYERS FOR PART OF ITS CCB STAKE

Wall Street firms are scrambling to find buyers for a chunk of Bank of America's16.7% stake in China
Construction Bank, people familiar with the talks say.



'STAR TREK' BOLDLY LIFTS PARAMOUNT OUT OF SLUMP

Paramount's risky bet paid off as "Star Trek" beat industry predictions to sell an estimated $76.5 million of tickets in the U.S. since its opening Thursday evening, and the studio hopes it has beamed itself out of the creative doldrums.



PMI GROUP'S LOSS NARROWS

PMI reports a loss of $115.3 million, or $1.41 a share, compared with a year-earlier loss of $274 million, citing lower reserve additions and a year-ago hit related to the company's stake in Financial Guaranty Insurance Corp.



WELLCARE SWINGS TO LOSS ON LEGAL COSTS

WellCare Health posts a 1Q loss of $36.9 million, or 89c a share, due to legal costs from a Medicaid-fraud case and higher costs at its Medicare Advantage private fee-for-service plans. Excluding items results top expectations.

RELIANT ENERGY HAS LOSS AS REVENUE SLUMPS

Electricity supplier swings to a 1Q loss of $151.4 million, or 43c a share, as revenue drop 47% after company completes the sale of its Texas retail business. Reliant cuts its 2009 and 2010 earnings targets.



SOUTHERN UNION NET DOWN 44% ON HURRICANES

Natural gas company's 1Q earnings slump to $46.3 million, or 36c a share, on hurricane-related
charges and the impact of hedging, but the results top analysts' expectations due to increased margins.



ALLIED CAPITAL LOSS WIDENS ON INVESTMENT WOES

Allied Capital's reports a loss of $347.7 million, or $1.95 a share, from $40.7 million a year earlier. The leveraged-buyout financier has seen more than $1 billion in investment losses, resulting in red ink for each of the past five quarters.

ALCOA EXPECTS $4B IMPROVEMENT IN CASH-FLOW

Aluminum smelter Alcoa is targeting a $4 billion improvement in cash flow and capital expenditure of
$1.6 billion this year, vice president Bernt Reitan says.


Page 52 of 226 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.

VIRGIN MOBILE NET SOARS, BOOSTS VIEW

Earnings more than double for the pay-as-you-go wireless company. It posts quarter earnings of $13.5 million, or 19c a share, up from $4.8 million a year earlier. Excluding one-time items, the company had a profit of 24c a share.



AGRIUM AGAIN BOOSTS CASH PORTION OF CF BID

Agrium increases the cash portion of its hostile offer for rival fertilizer maker CF Industries to $40 a share from $35, increasing the overall value of its bid to $4.18 billion. CF shares rise 3% premarket, Agrium shares fall 1%.


INTEL TO GET EU ANTITRUST FINE
The European Commission on Wednesday will  fine the world's biggest computer-chip maker,  Intel, for breaking European antitrust rules, people familiar with the matter say.



GLOBAL CENTRAL BANKERS CAUTIOUSLY OPTIMISTIC

Global central bankers say that the international economic downturn is easing, but caution against too much optimism that the economy will rapidly return to healthy growth.



SANTOS TO RAISE UP TO $2.3B FOR LNG PROJECT

Santos says it plans to raise up to $2.3 billion from a deeply discounted equity raising and may sell a stake in its Gladstone liquefied natural gas project to help fund its gas export strategy.



MACQUARIE SAID FINALIZING BID FOR AIG UNIT

Macquarie Group is finalizing a bid for the fund-management arm of American International Group, the
Australian newspaper reports without citing its sources.



BENETTON NET FALLS 38%, GIVES NO GUIDANCE

Apparel retailer Benetton Group reports a less-than-expected 38% fall in 1Q net profit, as shoppers worldwide continue to hold back spending. The Italian company doesn't give any guidance for 2009.

TOP STORIES

WHITE HOUSE RAISES DEFICIT PROJECTIONS

Changes in a host of tax and spending projections propel the federal deficit higher, primarily the result of the continued economic woes. Deficit will climb to a record $1.841 trillion this fiscal year and the White House expects the gap to be $1.258 trillion in fiscal 2010.

TD



US STOCKS OPEN LOWER

U.S. stocks open broadly lower, led by declines in financials, consumer and energy stocks, as traders take profits after last week's strong gains that saw the S&P 500 rise nearly 6%. The DJIA is down 100 points, while the S&P 500 is off 14 points, or 1.5%.



RAFT OF FINANCIAL FIRMS UNVEIL STOCK SALES

A handful of financial companies announce plans to collectively sell more than $7 billion in stock as they look to raise cash during a period of investor interest, with some companies using the proceeds to pay back funds from the TARP.

5 KILLED IN SHOOTING AT US BASE IN IRAQ

Five U.S. soldiers are killed in a shooting at U.S. base Camp Liberty Baghdad Monday, the military said in a statement.

Stress-Test Postmortem: Building Confidence

HSBC SAYS 1Q 'RESILIENT'
HSBC says that it remains "well-positioned" for the "highly uncertain environment" after its performance in the 1Q bounced back, continuing the trend of banks reporting strong performance in investment banking operations.

TD


CENTRICA BUYS 20% OF BRITISH ENERGY
U.K. utility Centrica says it has bought a 20% stake in EDF's U.K. nuclear unit British Energy in a deal worth GBP2.3 billion and says it expects good profit growth in 2009 in line with expectations.

LONMIN SWINGS TO LOSS, LAUNCHES $457M RIGHTS ISSUE
Shares in Lonmin fall after the world's third-biggest platinum miner posted a 1H loss and announced a rights issue to raise $457 million. Platinum prices have halved since peaking in March 2008.

TUI NET PROFIT SOARS ON DISPOSALS
German tourism and shipping company TUI says it expects to make a full-year profit after swinging a net gain in the first quarter, helped by the sale of its container shipping unit Hapag-Lloyd, but its quarterly net result was below analysts' forecasts. --TUI Travel Says Well Placed For 2009



EUROPEAN STOCKS MODERATELY LOWER
European stocks open moderately lower, as investors pause to take profits from last week's rally and rethink their risk strategies. Earlier, Asian shares turned mostly lower as investors sold stocks following earlier gains.



STATOILHYDRO NET FALLS 77%, PLANS COST CUTS
StatoilHydro says it will continue to pursue further cost reductions as it reports a 77% slide in net profit due to sharply lower oil prices and a financial items loss.

ASTRAZENECA'S BRILINTA DRUG BEATS PLAVIX IN TRIAL
AstraZeneca says its experimental blood thinner Brilinta outperformed Sanofi-Aventis' and Bristol-Myers Squibb's blockbuster Plavix in a key clinical trial with heart patients, boosting shares in the U.K.-based drug maker.



UK FACES BILL TO CANCEL EUROFIGHTER ORDER
Cancellation of the U.K.'s latest order of Eurofighter Typhoon jets could cost the U.K. more than GBP2 billion in penalties and leave the country facing additional bills, a newspaper reports, citing senior government sources.



BT TO AXE 10,000 JOBS AS PENSION GAP HITS GBP8B
Page 59 of 226 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
BT will axe a further 10,000 jobs as its pension gap hits GBP8 billion, which could force it to pay up to
GBP600 million annually to make up the shortfall, a U.K. daily reports, without citing sources.



TRAVIS PERKINS LAUNCHES GBP300M RIGHTS ISSUE
Travis Perkins becomes the latest U.K. company to launch a cash call from shareholders as it reports a further slump in sales for the first four months of the year, though the performance is better than the company had feared.


INTEL TO GET EU ANTITRUST FINE
The European Commission on Wednesday will  fine the world's biggest computer-chip maker,  Intel, for breaking European antitrust rules, people familiar with the matter say.



MUFG BUYS MORGAN STANLEY COMMON STOCK
Mitsubishi UFJ Financial Group says that it will acquire $600 million Morgan Stanley common shares, bringing its stake in the U.S. investment bank to over 20%.



CITIC BANK TO BUY STAKE IN AFFILIATE FOR $1.74B
China Citic Bank says it is buying a 70.32% stake in Citic International Financial Holdings from its parent for $1.74 billion as part of its plans for foreign expansion.

COMMERZBANK SEEKS BUYERS FOR KLEINWORT
Germany has forced Commerzbank to sell its U.K. private bank, Kleinwort, after supplying it with EUR18.2 billion state aid. Schroders, Standard Chartered and Canadian bank RBC are among the most likely bidders, sources say.



BARCAP GOES ON EUROPEAN HIRING SPREE
Barclays Capital, the investment banking arm of Barclays, is on a hiring spree looking for another 100 bankers to build up its merger and acquisition activities as well as its European equities business, a press report says.

U.K. utility Centrica says it has bought a 20% stake in EDF's U.K. nuclear unit British Energy in a deal worth GBP2.3 billion and says it expects good profit growth in 2009 in line with expectations. --EDF Says No Need To Raise Capital For Centrica Deal

LONMIN SWINGS TO LOSS, LAUNCHES $457M RIGHTS ISSUE
Shares in Lonmin fall after the world's third-biggest platinum miner posted a 1H loss and announced a rights issue to raise $457 million. Platinum prices have halved since peaking in March 2008.

TUI NET PROFIT SOARS ON DISPOSALS
German tourism and shipping company TUI says it expects to make a full-year profit after swinging a net gain in the first quarter, helped by the sale of its container shipping unit Hapag-Lloyd, but its quarterly net result was below analysts' forecasts. --TUI Travel Says Well Placed For 2009



RIO CHAIRMAN TO MEET UK SHAREHOLDERS
Rio Tinto confirms that new Chairman Jan du Plessis will start meeting U.K. shareholders this week as the Anglo-Australian miner continues to gauge investor reaction to a proposed $19.5 billion investment from Chinalco.



STATOILHYDRO NET FALLS 77%, PLANS COST CUTS
StatoilHydro says it will continue to pursue further cost reductions as it reports a 77% slide in net profit due to sharply lower oil prices and a financial items loss.



DNO SHARES UP 20% ON IRAQ EXPORT PERMIT
Shares in DNO International rise nearly 20%, after it received permission from the Kurdish regional government in Iraq to start exporting crude oil from the Tawke field from June 1.

ALCOA EXPECTS $4B IMPROVEMENT IN CASH-FLOW
Aluminum smelter Alcoa is targeting a $4 billion improvement in cash flow and capital expenditure of
$1.6 billion this year, vice president Bernt Reitan says.

ASTRAZENECA'S BRILINTA DRUG BEATS PLAVIX IN TRIAL


Page 63 of 226 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
AstraZeneca says its experimental blood thinner Brilinta outperformed Sanofi-Aventis' and
Bristol-Myers Squibb's blockbuster Plavix in a key clinical trial with heart patients, boosting shares in
the U.K.-based drug maker.



UK FACES BILL TO CANCEL EUROFIGHTER ORDER
Cancellation of the U.K.'s latest order of Eurofighter Typhoon jets could cost the U.K. more than GBP2 billion in penalties and leave the country facing additional bills, a newspaper reports, citing senior government sources.



TRAVIS PERKINS LAUNCHES GBP300M RIGHTS ISSUE
Travis Perkins becomes the latest U.K. company to launch a cash call from shareholders as it reports a further slump in sales for the first four months of the year, though the performance is better than the company had feared.


INTEL TO GET EU ANTITRUST FINE
The European Commission on Wednesday will  fine the world's biggest computer-chip maker,  Intel, for breaking European antitrust rules, people familiar with the matter say.



MUFG BUYS MORGAN STANLEY COMMON STOCK
Mitsubishi UFJ Financial Group says that it will acquire $600 million Morgan Stanley common shares, bringing its stake in the U.S. investment bank to over 20%.

HD      EU to rule in Intel antitrust case on Wednesday:  source
WC     288 words
PD      11 May 2009
ET      02:41 PM
SN      Agence France Presse
SC      AFPR
LA      English
CY      Copyright Agence France-Presse, 2009 All reproduction and presentation rights reserved.

LP
The European Commission is to rule Wednesday on whether US computer chip giant  Intel has abused its market power to crush rivals unfairly, a source close to the matter said.
A commission spokesman declined to comment on the matter on Monday.

TD
If found guilty of trying to thwart competitors illegally, the Santa Clara, California-based company could face fines running of 10 percent of its annual worldwide revenue, which stood at 37.6 billion dollars (51 billion dollars) in 2008.
The Financial Times newspaper reported Monday that the fine could top the record penalties that
Brussels has slapped on Microsoft in its long-running antitrust battle with the US software giant.

The commission originally  finedMicrosoft 497 million euros (674 million dollars) for abusing its dominating grip on the market for personal computer operating systems.

However, five years of legal battles and an unsuccessful appeal later, the  fines have reached 1.676 billion euros, although Microsoft has appealed against part of the sum.

The European Commission filed antitrust charges against Intel in July 2007 accusing the company of offering "substantial" rebates to computer makers that mostly used its chips.
In July 2008, it lodged a fresh complaint, charging  Intel of offering discounts to a major European
personal computer distributor to favour its products, paid a PC maker to delay marketing a model line with
a rival's chips, and also paid it to use  Intel's own microprocessors in preference.

Intel's chief competitor Advanced Micro Devices has long accused Intel of using its grip on the market for microchips -- the brains of personal computers -- to choke off competition.

Page 68 of 226 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.


HD         Corporate  News: Intel to Get EU Antitrust Fine
BY          By Peppi Kiviniemi
WC         376 words
PD          11 May 2009
SN          The Wall Street Journal
SC          J
PG         B2
LA          English
CY          (Copyright (c) 2009, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

LP
BRUSSELS -- The European Commission on Wednesday will  fine the world's biggest computer-chip maker, Intel Corp., for breaking European antitrust rules, people familiar with the matter said Friday.

The commission, the executive arm of the European Union, will also tell  Intel to make changes to the way it provides rebates to computer makers, the people said.

TD
Commission officials spent most of Friday discussing the  Intel fine with national  competition authorities, which according to EU procedure also have a say on the matter, the people familiar with the case said.

They added that once all the stakeholders have had their say on the  fine, the final document will be presented to the college of commissioners Wednesday, which is then likely to adopt it.

An Intel spokesman declined to comment, except to say that word of the pending decision amounts to rumors and speculation. A commission spokesman declined to comment.

Intel's trouble with the commission dates from 2000, when chip-maker Advanced Micro Devices Inc., of Sunnyvale, Calif., filed a complaint saying Intel was blocking its access to the market. In 2007, the EU sent preliminary charges to  Intel, alleging Intel kept AMD out of the microprocessor market by paying computer makers rebates to keep their usage of AMD chips down and by selling chips below cost to thwart AMD from swooping on key accounts. Last year, the EU added more charges, alleging  Intel paid a major retailer to keep computers that use AMD chips off its shelves.

The commission's decision set for Wednesday is said to be extremely complex and lengthy, in order to safeguard the antitrust enforcer against any possible legal challenges from  Intel, which is likely facing one of the highest fines in Europe's  antitrust history, according to Brussels-based lawyers.
EU competition commissioner Neelie Kroes can, under commission's rules,  fine Intel as much as
10% of its annual revenue, which in 2008 was almost $38 billion. Last year, the commission issued a
record $1.16 billion  fine against Microsoft Corp. for noncompliance in the EU's long-running  antitrust
fight with the software giant.
---
Charles Forelle contributed to this article.

HD       FACTBOX-Five facts on Intel vs AMD rivalry
WC      587 words
PD       11 May 2009
ET        03:28 PM
SN       Reuters News
SC       LBA
LA        English
CY       (c) 2009 Reuters Limited

LP
May 11 (Reuters) - EU  antitrust regulators are expected to say this week that  Intel Corp illegally paid computer makers to postpone or cancel the launch of PCs containing chips made by rival Advanced Micro Devices . [ID:nLA487575]
For a Timeline on the Commission's case against  Intel, click on [ID:nLB111652]

TD
Following are five facts on the rivalry between the two:
MARKET SHARE

Last year Intel made 80.5 percent of the microprocessors that power personal computers, gaining share each quarter, helped by the success of its Atom chip in the emerging netbook sector and by the continued strength of its brands and products in the desktop, notebook and server segments.
AMD had 12 percent of the market.

The firms' combined market share rose last year for the third year in a row to 92.5 percent, compared with 90.9 percent in 2006, according to research firm iSuppli.
RELATIVE SIZE

Intel, based in Santa Clara, California, made revenues of $37.6 billion in 2008. It employed 83,900 staff at the end of 2008, more than half in the United States. Its market value is $85.4 billion and it is the world's biggest chip maker.

AMD, based in Sunnyvale, California, made revenues of $5.8 billion in 2008. It employs about 11,000 people and has a market value of $2.6 billion.
CASE IN BRUSSELS

The European Commission has already said Intel's pricing practices -- in particular rebates to computer makers and retailers -- were aimed at driving AMD out of the market.

In two formal charge sheets laid out in July 2007 and July 2008, the Commission said  Intel gave rebates to computer makers so long as they agreed to obtain most or all of their processor-chips from  Intel and made payments to induce computer makers either to delay or cancel the launch of products using AMD chips.

The U.S. tech group has denied the charges that it was abusing its market dominance, arguing its conduct had been lawful and beneficial to clients and consumers.
CASES ELSEWHERE

In December 2008 Intel filed a formal complaint to overturn an earlier South Korean  antitrust ruling that it had abused its dominant market position. [ID:nSEO42148]

Intel said in a statement its filing to the Seoul High Court asserted that the Korea Fair Trade Commission
(KFTC) made substantial factual and legal errors in formulating its final opinion.
Page 73 of 226 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
The KFTC said in June 2008 that  Intel offered rebates to South Korean personal computer makers in return for their not buying microprocessors from AMD.  Intel was also fined.

The U.S. Federal Trade Commission launched a probe into  Intel after South Korean authorities  fined it for offering rebates in return for not buying AMD products.

The group faces more legal woes as a U.S. lawsuit brought by AMD is set to go to court in 2010.  Intel also faces dozens of separate class actions, filed in various U.S. courts, modelled on the AMD complaint.
U.S. LICENCE BATTLE

In March 2009 Intel accused AMD of breaching the terms of a cross-licensing agreement between the two, a charge that AMD denied. [ID:nN16422958]

Intel alleged its rival ran foul of a 2001 patent agreement when it created the Globalfoundries joint venture, the $4.3 billion semiconductor manufacturer spun off from AMD. (Compiled by Tarmo Virki, editing by Will Waterman)

Page 74 of 226 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.


HD         TIMELINE-Intel's  antitrust battle with the EU WC        409 words
PD         11 May 2009
ET          03:28 PM
SN         Reuters News
SC         LBA
LA          English
CY         (c) 2009 Reuters Limited

LP
May 11 (Reuters) - The  European Commission is set to rule on Wednesday that  Intel , the world's largest microchip maker, illegally paid computer manufacturers to restrict their use of chips made by rival AMD . [ID:nLA487575]
For a Factbox on the  Intel-AMD rivalry click [ID:nLB705099]

TD
The Commission, tasked with ensuring companies do not  abuse any market dominance or make agreements that restrict  competition in the 27-country European Union, is expected to  fine Intel and order a halt to some of the rebates it gives to computer makers.

U.S.-based  Intel's central processing units power four out of five of the world's 1 billion personal computers and servers.
Here are the key events in  Intel's antitrust battle with the Brussels-based Commission.

2001 - The Commission launches an investigation into an AMD complaint that  Intel used unfair business practices to persuade companies to buy its microprocessors.

July 2007 - The Commission accuses  Intel of abusing its dominant position with marketing and pricing practices that infringe EU laws.
January 2008 - Intel responds to the charge sheet, known as a "statement of objections". February 2008 - The Commission inspects documents at  Intel's Feldkirchen office in Germany. March 2008 - Intel responds orally to the charges at a hearing held by the EU executive.
July 2008 - The Commission sends a supplementary statement of objections to Intel, accusing it of giving rebates to a major European PC retailer on condition that the firm sell only Intel-based PCs. The EU executive also files two other charges related to computer makers.

October 2008 -  Intel files an appeal with the Court of First Instance in Luxembourg, asking the EU's second-highest court to overrule decisions by the Commission that it says limit the evidence available to the company to conduct its defence.
January 2009 - The court rejects  Intel's appeal.
February 2009 - Intel responds to the Commission's supplementary statement of objections. March 2009 - The Commission asks  Intel for information on its global and European revenues. April 2009 - Intel provides the revenue details.

TOP STORIES

BANKS ANNOUNCE SHARE SALES TO REPAY TARP

A number of banks announce plans to raise cash by selling shares and to pay back funds from the Troubled Asset Relief Program, including Capital One, U.S. Bancorp and Principal Financial Group. Wells Faro plans to sell 341 million shares after being told by the U.S. Treasury to boost its capital base.

TD



US STOCK FUTURES DROP AFTER STRONG WEEK

U.S. stock futures are lower, suggesting stocks are a little fatigued after another sprint higher last week. The S&P 500 is now up more than 37% from its March 9 low. Thin menu for economic data until Wednesday's reports on April retail sales and import prices and March business inventories.



US PLAYED ROUGH WITH CHRYSLER'S CREDITORS

President Barack Obama's auto task force heard a blunt message early this spring from J.P. Morgan Chase & Co., the largest lender to Chrysler. In any deal to remake the troubled auto maker, Chrysler would have to repay its lenders all $6.9 billion it owed.



HEDGE FUNDS RAISE VOICES, AND RETURNS

Hedge-fund managers are showing rare public outrage against the Obama administration, saying that it has wrongly rebuked investors necessary to salving the financial crisis.



DISH NETWORK PROFIT RISES 21%; ECHOSTAR SWINGS TO LOSS

Dish Network says its 1Q earnings rose to $312.7 million, or 70c a share, despite another drop in subscribers, while former unit EchoStar swung to a loss of $645,000, or a penny a share, on falling revenue.



ACKMAN MAKES CASE TO CHANGE TARGET BOARD


Page 82 of 226 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Hedge-fund mogul calls a meeting in Manhattan today to introduce his slate of five dissident directors, including himself, that he's asking shareholders to elect May 28.



GM HIRES SEARCH FIRM FOR NEW BOARD

General Motors has hired an executive-search firm to help find replacements for at least half of its 12 directors, reflecting the Obama administration's increasing influence over the auto maker.



BOFA SEEKS BUYERS FOR PART OF ITS CCB STAKE

Wall Street firms are scrambling to find buyers for a chunk of Bank of America's16.7% stake in China
Construction Bank, people familiar with the talks say.



'STAR TREK' BOLDLY LIFTS PARAMOUNT OUT OF SLUMP

Paramount's risky bet paid off as "Star Trek" beat industry predictions to sell an estimated $76.5 million of tickets in the U.S. since its opening Thursday evening, and the studio hopes it has beamed itself out of the creative doldrums.



PMI GROUP'S LOSS NARROWS

PMI reports a loss of $115.3 million, or $1.41 a share, compared with a year-earlier loss of $274 million, citing lower reserve additions and a year-ago hit related to the company's stake in Financial Guaranty Insurance Corp.



OBAMA AIDE TO GUIDE OVERHAUL WITH LIGHT TOUCH

The cost-cutting pledge to be announced by health-care groups is an example of the White House strategy that health czar Nancy-Ann DeParle has pushed, namely keeping potential opponents on board.



WELLCARE SWINGS TO LOSS ON LEGAL COSTS

WellCare Health posts a 1Q loss of $36.9 million, or 89c a share, due to legal costs from a Medicaid-fraud case and higher costs at its Medicare Advantage private fee-for-service plans. Excluding items results top expectations.



CREDIT SUISSE PLANS NEW JAPANESE TRADING SERVICE

Credit Suisse is launching a service that will allow clients to trade through Japan's alternative equities markets as well as the main Tokyo exchange, a move intended to boost sluggish growth in Asia's new trading venues.

ALCOA EXPECTS $4B IMPROVEMENT IN CASH-FLOW

Aluminum smelter Alcoa is targeting a $4 billion improvement in cash flow and capital expenditure of
$1.6 billion this year, vice president Bernt Reitan says.


INTEL TO GET EU ANTITRUST FINE
The European Commission on Wednesday will  fine the world's biggest computer-chip maker,  Intel, for breaking European antitrust rules, people familiar with the matter say.


Page 83 of 226 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.

GLOBAL CENTRAL BANKERS CAUTIOUSLY OPTIMISTIC

Global central bankers say that the international economic downturn is easing, but caution against too much optimism that the economy will rapidly return to healthy growth.



DEATH TOLL RISES AS IRAQ SLOWS SURGE

Violence is on the rise in Iraq as U.S. troops withdraw. A ground-level look at the handover provides one explanation: The Iraqi government is neglecting many of the successful counterinsurgency initiatives it is inheriting from the U.S. military.



SANTOS TO RAISE UP TO $2.3B FOR LNG PROJECT

Santos says it plans to raise up to $2.3 billion from a deeply discounted equity raising and may sell a stake in its Gladstone liquefied natural gas project to help fund its gas export strategy.



POPE URGES ISRAELI-PALESTINIAN COMPROMISE

Pope Benedict XVI lands in Israel, urging Israelis and Palestinians to reach a compromise on a two-state solution to their conflict and denouncing the persistence of anti-Semitism worldwide.



MACQUARIE SAID FINALIZING BID FOR AIG UNIT

Macquarie Group is finalizing a bid for the fund-management arm of American International Group, the
Australian newspaper reports without citing its sources.



BIG CELLPHONE MAKERS MUSCLE INTO THE LOW END

The U.S. cellphone market is poised for another round of consolidation as large manufacturers turn their attention to the growing prepaid market, pressuring smaller rivals.



UN DENOUNCES SRI LANKAN CIVILIAN 'BLOODBATH'

The U.N. condemns the killing of civilians in Sri Lanka at the weekend as a "bloodbath" in which more than 100 children died, as the government and rebels trade blame for the attacks.



FIREFIGHTERS SEE VICTORY AGAINST CALIF. WILDFIRE

After days of high temperatures and powerful winds, a moist layer of marine air shrouds the coastal town of Santa Barbara, leaving fire chiefs confident of reaching full containment of the fire by Wednesday.

BERKSHIRE HATHAWAY POSTS 1Q LOSS

Berkshire Hathaway reports a loss of $1.53 billion, or $990 a Class A share, despite solid growth in operating earnings at its insurance operations, as investment losses continued to mount. Operating EPS comes to $1,100, down from $1,249.

HD     'Intel facing fine from EU - report' WC    219 words
PD     11 May 2009
SN     DMEurope
SC     DMEUR
LA      English
CY     © 2009 DMEurope. All rights reserved.

LP
The European Commission will fine Intel for breaking European competition rules, people familiar with the matter told the Wall Street Journal. The commission will also tell Intel to make changes to the way it provides rebates to computer makers, the paper's sources said. Commission officials spent most of 8 May discussing the Intel fine with national  competition authorities, which according to EU procedure also
have a say on the matter. Once all the stakeholders have had their say on the  fine, the final document will be presented to the college of commissioners 13 May, which is then likely to adopt it. Spokesmen for  Intel and the EC declined to comment.  Intel's trouble with the commission dates from 2000, when AMD filed a complaint saying Intel was blocking its access to the market.

TD
In 2007, the EU sent preliminary charges to  Intel, alleging Intel kept AMD out of the microprocessor market by paying computer makers rebates to keep their usage of AMD chips down and by selling chips below cost to thwart AMD from swooping on key accounts. Last year, the EU added more charges, alleging Intel paid a major retailer to keep computers that use AMD chips off its shelves.

HD        US signals  tougher  line on dominant firms
BY        By Tom Braithwaite in Washington
WC       605 words
PD        11 May 2009
SN        Financial Times (FT.Com)
SC        FTCOM
LA         English
CY        (c) 2009 The Financial Times Limited. All rights reserved

LP
US companies will face much more  antitrust scrutiny from the Department of Justice, reversing an eight-year period in which the Bush administration drew back from tackling alleged anti-competitive behaviour.

Christine Varney, head of the Department of Justice's  antitrust division, on Monday said she was revoking a Bush administration anti-trust doctrine that advocated "extreme hesitancy in the face of potential abuses by  monopoly firms.

TD
"In a free society government must intervene when  competition is threatened," she said, adding that continuing a minimal approach to tackling monopolistic behaviour was the wrong path, even in a financial crisis.

"It's clear we have a new sheriff in town," said Ed Black, a lawyer and head of the Computer & Communications Industry Association. "There's so much that was left ignored by the last administration, so a main job will be catching up."

The tabular content relating to this article is not available to view. Apologies in advance for the inconvenience caused.

A former lawyer specialising in the internet, Ms Varney's appointment and apparent willingness to scrutinise large technology companies could concern Google,  Intel, Microsoft and IBM, all of which have faced previous antitrust action.
The European Commission is expected on Wednesday to levy a  fine for anti-competitive behaviour on
Intel, the chipmaker, that could top €1bn ($1.36bn, £897m).

With complainants focusing on Europe as a more active venue for  antitrust cases, Ms Varney said she would "discourage any company from forum shopping" as the DoJ would now be "co-operating closely" with the Commission.

Lawyers said Ms Varney's change of approach would see more alignment between the US and Europe after eight years in which the Commission continued to attack perceived anti-competitive behaviour with large fines while the Bush administration did not follow suit.
"The EU and US in the 1990s were operating in parallel," said Mr Black. "The US took a detour [under
George W. Bush] whereas the EU has really stayed on the mainline track."

In a speech to the liberal Center for American Progress, Ms Varney said the financial crisis was a reason to step up  antitrust activity not to pull back. She raised the question as to whether companies perceived as "too big to fail", which present risk to the financial system, could have had their growth blocked by antitrust action.
She said her team would examine the use of government stimulus funds to try to prevent fraud.

"In the event that preventive efforts fail, we will be there to investigate and swiftly prosecute individuals and entities responsible for criminal  antitrust violations." Sean Heather, a lawyer at the US Chamber of


Page 91 of 226 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Commerce, the largest US business group, said he was against "inappropriate"  antitrust action but saw no reason to believe Ms Varney would pursue a "radical" agenda.

He said the biggest danger in international  antitrust law was European Union and US disagreement opening the door to a "third way" approach from countries such as China, whose developing business law could threaten overseas companies trying to make acquisitions.

While the Bush administration did not pursue companies alleged to have over-extended themselves with vertical integration - buying up parts of their own supply chain - the former Department of Justice did
take a hard line on cartel activity, which was judged to be more clear-cut  abuse of the law.

But in a key report published in September and revoked on Monday by Ms Varney, the administration had said there should be no interference in "the rough and tumble of beneficial  competition."

HD        US to get tough on competition issues
BY        By Tom Braithwaite in Washington
WC       607 words
PD        11 May 2009
SN        Financial Times (FT.Com)
SC        FTCOM
LA         English
CY        (c) 2009 The Financial Times Limited. All rights reserved

LP
The US is to take a more interventionist approach to  competition issues, reversing the stance of
George W. Bush's administration and moving closer to the way that European regulators operate.

Christine Varney, head of the Department of Justice's  antitrust division, said on Monday she was revoking a Bush administration antitrust doctrine that she said advocated "extreme hesitancy in the face of potential abuses by monopoly firms".

TD
"In a free society government must intervene when  competition is threatened," she said, adding that continuing a minimal approach to tackling monopolistic behaviour was the wrong path, even in a financial crisis.

"It's clear we have a new sheriff in town," said Ed Black, a lawyer and head of the Computer & Communications Industry Association. "There's so much that was left ignored by the last administration, so a main job will be catching up."

A former lawyer specialising in the internet, Ms Varney's appointment and apparent willingness to scrutinise large technology companies may concern Google,  Intel, Microsoft and IBM, all of which have faced antitrust action. The European Commission is expected on Wednesday to levy a  fine for
anti-competitive behaviour on Intel, the chipmaker, that could top €1bn ($1.36bn).

The tabular content relating to this article is not available to view. Apologies in advance for the inconvenience caused.

With complainants focusing on Europe for  antitrust cases, Ms Varney said she would "discourage any company from forum shopping" as the DoJ would now be "co-operating closely" with the commission.

In a speech to the liberal Center for American Progress, Ms Varney said the financial crisis was a reason to step up  antitrust activity and not to pull back.
She asked whether companies regarded as "too big to fail" could have had their growth blocked by
antitrust action.
Ms Varney said her team would examine the use of government stimulus funds to try to prevent fraud. "In the event that preventive efforts fail, we will be there to investigate and swiftly prosecute individuals
and entities responsible for criminal  antitrust violations."

She said there would be no impediment to the DoJ tackling  competition abuses at companies that had received government equity stakes in the wake of the financial crisis. Those direct equity stakes are expected to grow as the government converts preferred equity stakes in banks into common stock.

Although Ms Varney was keen to stress that she saw her appointment as a break with the past she also indicated that there would be no witch-hunt of companies. "We need to be careful not to swing back too far the other way," she said.




Page 93 of 226 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Some lawyers said the impact of tougher enforcement from the Department of Justice could be mitigated in court where most complaints about monopolies were brought by private companies rather than the government.

"Since roughly the late 1970s, courts have adopted rules that have appeared to make it difficult for plaintiffs to win their monopolisation claims against dominant firms," said Keith Hylton of the Boston University School of Law.
"That has nothing to do with the Bush people."

While the Bush administration did not pursue companies alleged to have over-extended themselves with vertical integration - buying up their own supply chain - the DoJ did take a hard line on cartel activity, which was judged to be a more clear-cut  abuse of the law.

But in a key report published in September and revoked on Monday by Ms Varney, the administration had said there should be no interference in "the rough and tumble of beneficial  competition".

HD        Intel braced for billion-euro fine under European antitrust rules
BY        By Nikki Tait in Brussels and Chris Nuttall in San Francisco
WC       432 words
PD        11 May 2009
SN        Financial Times
SC        FTFT
ED        USA Ed2
PG       01
LA        English

EU commissioners to approve action

Intel, the world's biggest chipmaker by sales, is bracing itself for one of the heaviest penalties levied in Europe for anti-competitive behaviour. The European Union is expected formally to approve action against Intel at its weekly meeting of commissioners on Wednesday.

TD
It will be the climax of a near-decade-long investigation into the group's marketing practices. Lawyers say the fine could top those heaped on Microsoft, which, combined, exceeded €1bn ($1.36bn).

The Intel investigation dates back to 2000, when its closest rival, Advanced Micro Devices, filed a complaint that it was being driven out of the market by  Intel's practices.
Four years later, the case gathered steam and, after a series of raids,  Intel was formally charged in July
2007. It had allegedly abused its dominant market position and offered illegal rebates to computer
manufacturers, which had shut AMD out of the market.

After further raids last year, the European Commission accused Intel of using rebates to persuade a leading European retailer to sell only Intel-based personal computers. Intel has consistently denied behaving illegally. "Our business practices are lawful, pro-competitive and good for consumers," it says.

In deciding how to shape and enforce a "cease-and-desist" order, the EU regulator will confront a web of incentives and rebates offered by  Intel to customers that have helped it dominate the market for PC microprocessor chips.

Opinions are divided on how specific the Commission will be. In theory, if its final decision goes against the company, the  fine could be as much as 10 per cent of  Intel's annual worldwide revenues. These totalled $37.6bn in 2008, meaning the maximum level could approach $4bn. However, that 10 per cent level is rarely reached.

Intel has refused to discuss whether it would appeal against an adverse decision.

After Japan's fair trade commission ruled in 2005 that  Intel had abused its monopoly power, the company heeded a cease-and-desist order, but did not accept guilt or pay a fine.

Last June, South Korea's fair trade commission  fined Intel Won26bn ($21m) for abusing its dominant market position there and offering discounts to two Korean PC makers to drive AMD out of the market. This decision is under appeal.  Intel had $10.3bn in cash and securities at the end of the first quarter.

SE        Investing
HD       3 Stock Picks: COF, AMD, VM BY        By Will Swarts
WC      799 words
PD       11 May 2009
ET        06:01 AM
SN       SmartMoney.com
SC       SMRTO
LA        English
CY       © 2009 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

LP
Capital One Financial, fresh off a clean bill of health after the release of the government’s stress test last week, headed a quartet of financial companies on Monday that said they would sell shares to repay funds borrowed from the Treasury Department’s Troubled Asset Relief Program (TARP). The McLean, Va.-based credit-card lender said it would issue 56 million shares, priced at $27.75, in an effort to raise about $1.6 billion. Shares dropped 10% during early trading on the expectation of shareholder dilution.

"Net proceeds of the offering are expected to be used for general corporate purposes and, subject to regulatory approval, for the repurchase of the preferred stock and warrants issued and sold, in each case, to the U.S. Treasury as part of the Troubled Asset Relief Program Capital Purchase Program," the company said.

TD
Principal Financial Group, US Bancorp and BB&T Corp also announced share sales.

Analysts have been waiting for a time when the company could put the stress test behind it and investors would start to evaluate the firm on more traditional metrics. Now that it's happened, SunTrust Robinson Humphrey analyst John Stilmar, writing last week before the official results were known (but after numerous leaks found their way to news outlets), said these developments make Capital One a more attractive prospect "as few market participants believed COF would sail through the stress test unscathed."

Bottom Line: Hold The financial sector is too shaky even after the stress tests to be a comfortable space for small investors.
Court Ruling Boosts AMD

One of Silicon Valley's biggest battles ended on Monday in Brussels when the  European Commission said it would fine chip maker Intel for antitrust violations, nine years after competitor Advanced Micro Devices brought suit.

Shares of AMD popped Monday on the news.  Intel didn't immediately comment on the announcement, though it waged a long legal battle with the commission.  Intel last issued a statement in January objecting to the commission's refusal to seek documents from AMD that  Intel claimed could help its case.

"Certain AMD documents were made part of the record in the European proceeding and Intel sought to demonstrate that those documents indicated that other highly relevant documents existed. Intel asked the Commission to order AMD to provide those documents and the Commission refused," the company said on Jan. 27.

Intel could face fines of up to 10% of its revenue for allegedly blocking access to the European market. The company had revenue of $38 billion last year. Such a  fine could top the record $1.2 billion  fine issued in 2008 to Microsoft.

The legal boost comes after AMD posted solid quarterly results and investor opinion is swinging around to the idea that its Foundry chip-making spinoff has succeeded.
Page 99 of 226 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Analyst and head of Kintisheff Research Tsvetan Kintisheff on Friday upgraded the stock to Buy from
Hold based on its strong quarterly results and improving market conditions.
"The demand environment clearly is stabilizing and signs of this stability are abounding," he said.

Bottom Line: Buy AMD may still be second fiddle to  Intel, but the chip cycle and legal triumph make it an attractive option right now.
Virgin Mobile Rings Up Earnings
Investors bought shares of Virgin Mobile USA when the wireless carrier beat Wall Street estimates. While first quarter revenue of $337.3 million was short of Street consensus at $358 million, Virgin’s
profits of 24 cents a share easily beat estimates of 10 cents a share. It also raised guidance for the full
year, putting its expected profit at $127 million to $142 million, up from $117 million to $132 million.

Virgin has been trying to keep customers amid a downturn in consumer spending. It has found some success with turning on customers to flat-rate, contract-free wireless service. "As budgets tightened and consumers continued to optimize their minutes of use, it became readily apparent that our traditional lower-end prepaid customer lifetime value was increasingly marginal,” said CEO Dan Schulman on a Monday conference call. “The good news is the economic environment highlighted to our prepaid customers the inherent value and flexibility we provide and their churn rate stabilized and even begun to improve.”

Churn, or the total loss of customers, was at 4.8% for the quarter, in line with Thomas Weisel Partners analyst James Breen's estimate. "Despite the low gross additions this quarter, management saw an uptick in March and expects gross additions to improve in [the second half of 2009]," he wrote Monday.

Bottom Line: Hold Virgin doesn’t have to maintain a mobile network, which keeps its costs down, but its customer base could be vulnerable if the recession continues.

HD     Tech firms could see fallout  from antitrust shift
BY     By JORDAN ROBERTSON
CR     AP Technology Writer
WC    901 words
PD     12 May 2009
ET      06:15 AM
SN     Associated Press Newswires
SC     APRS
LA      English
CY     (c) 2009. The Associated Press. All Rights Reserved.

LP
SAN FRANCISCO (AP) - If the Obama administration is serious about more aggressively responding to antitrust complaints, some of technology's biggest companies may have to rethink their business strategies or expansion plans.

The administration said Monday it has abandoned policies of former President George W. Bush that it criticized as too friendly to companies that dominate their markets.

TD
The Justice Department didn't call out any companies by name, and its shift in approach will affect all industries. But it raises the stakes for tech heavyweights whose practices have been questioned elsewhere more than in the United States.

For instance,  Intel Corp. could face a steep  fine in Europe this week over its behavior in the microprocessor industry. An IBM Corp. competitor is accusing the company in Europe of manipulating the market for mainframe computers. Microsoft Corp. -- a marquee  antitrust defendant during the administration of President Bill Clinton -- has been battling other charges in Europe in this decade as well.

Tougher antitrust enforcement could also focus on Google Inc., whose leading market shares in online search and advertising markets were already drawing scrutiny in the waning days of the Bush administration.
It remains to be seen whether the Justice Department's different approach under President Barack
Obama will lead to more court cases or more challenges to proposed business deals.

But antitrust lawyers say a more aggressive antitrust philosophy likely would make the Obama administration resemble the Clinton years far more than the Bush years.

"This is back to the future," said Stephen Bomse, partner in the  antitrust group of the Orrick, Herrington & Sutcliffe law firm.

Under Clinton, the Justice Department brought 12 anti-monopoly cases that didn't involve mergers or acquisitions. That included the long-running case against Microsoft in which a judge at one point ordered Microsoft split into two. (The case eventually was settled with the Bush administration, though it lives on: The Justice Department recently extended its oversight of Microsoft's compliance with the settlement for another 18 months, into 2011).

Under Bush, the department brought three anti-monopoly cases, but none outside of mergers and acquisitions. The targets in those cases were Chicago-based industrial component supplier Amsted Industries Inc., newspaper publishers Daily Gazette Co. and MediaNews Group Inc., and chip maker Microsemi Corp.

Robert Pitofsky, chairman of the Federal Trade Commission from 1995 until 2001 and now an attorney with the Arnold & Porter law firm, said he doesn't think the U.S. will entirely match Europe's zeal for antitrust cases.


Page 101 of 226 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
One reason is that the U.S. is still more likely to have a free-market bent than Europe, he said. And it can be tricky to regulate technology from an  antitrust perspective. Technological innovations can create monopolists very quickly by cornering or creating specific parts of a market. If regulators are too aggressive, they run the risk of stifling innovation.

"The vast majority of activity in Silicon Valley is innovation, trying to make a better product -- that was not viewed as anticompetitive under Bush or Clinton," said Jonathan Jacobson, a partner in the  antitrust practice at the Wilson Sonsini Goodrich & Rosati law firm. "Technological innovation is supposed to keep you out of trouble."

But the standard that tech companies are judged by -- whether they are engaging in "exclusionary" or "predatory" conduct against competitors -- is the same as everyone else. Viewed through that lens, tech companies can be at a disadvantage because they grow so fast and can get on regulators' radars much more quickly.
Already fears of a tougher  antitrust regime under Obama might have played out in Silicon Valley.

When IBM was discussing a proposed $7 billion takeover of rival Sun Microsystems Inc. this spring, Sun sought detailed assurances that IBM would see the deal through an  antitrust review. When the talks collapsed, Sun ended up agreeing to be acquired by Oracle Corp., a company with less overlap with Sun, meaning the deal is perceived as less likely to generate  antitrust questions.

Intel's antitrust challenges arose from complaints by smaller rival Advanced Micro Devices Inc. According to AMD,  Intel boxed AMD out of certain accounts by paying computer makers and retailers to avoid AMD's products. AMD claims that  Intel would drop this hammer when AMD's market share would get too big.

Intel, which owns about 80 percent of the worldwide PC microprocessor market, argues that the rebates it offered were legal, and are a common way to reward buying in very high volumes.

European regulators don't buy  Intel's argument. They have charged  Intel with engaging in a coordinated strategy to limit AMD's reach, including by selling server chips below the cost of making them. The European Commission is expected to formally  fine Intel this week. The  fines can be up to 10 percent of a company's annual revenue, and in 2008,  Intel had $37.6 billion in worldwide sales.

The U.S. initially stayed on the sidelines while  Intel and AMD began duking it out overseas in 2003. Last year U.S. regulators stepped up their inquiries, with the Federal Trade Commission opening a formal probe into Intel's sales tactics. The FTC's two-year investigation had been considered "informal" until that point. So far, the U.S. Justice Department has not taken action.

HD      INTEL MAY FACE RECORD FINE, COMMISSION TIGHT-LIPPED WC    218 words
